Sunan of the Friday Khutbah: Acts and Proofs
Enumerating the sunan of the Friday khutbah with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the eighteen sunan acts of the Friday khutbah and explain the Hanafi proof that purity is sunnah, not a condition.

Core Idea
The khutbah is valid with any lengthy dhikr; its sunan are numerous but not obligatory.

The Friday khutbah is a key component of Jumu'ah prayer. This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi lists the recommended acts (sunan) related to the preacher and the sermon itself, citing the Quranic verse 'So hasten to the remembrance of Allah' and the incident of Sayyiduna Uthman to establish the minimum. Understanding these sunan helps distinguish essential conditions from recommended enhancements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal basis for the khutbah's minimum
Find the Quranic proof (62:9) and the incident of Uthman that show any lengthy dhikr suffices as a khutbah.
The word 'dhikr' is general, so it includes long and short remembrance.
2
List the sunan related to the khatib's state
Identify the sunan concerning the preacher: sitting in a chamber, wearing black/white, purity, covering private parts, and facing the people.
Purity is sunnah, not a condition, as clarified by the commentator.
3
List the sunan related to the khutbah performance
Enumerate the sunan of the act itself: sitting on minbar, adhan, iqamah, standing for two khutbahs, holding a sword in conquest towns, and beginning with praise, shahada, blessings, admonition, and Quranic verse.
Some acts like sitting are permissible but disliked without excuse.
4
Distinguish between sunnah and wajib
Note that all listed are sunnah muakkadah; omitting them is valid but disliked. Only the minimum dhikr is required.
Contrast with Shafi'i view that specifies pillars (arkan) for the khutbah.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider a khatib who delivers the khutbah without wudu. State the ruling based on the text.
The correct view: khutbah is valid but makruh without purity.

And supplication for the Muslims and tasbih, and the like are not called a khutbah. He has the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'So hasten to the remembrance of Allah,' without distinction between whether it is a long remembrance called a khutbah or not. And due to the incident of Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, when he said: 'Praise be to Allah' and then faltered, then he descended and led them in prayer, and no one among them objected to him, so it was a consensus from them. (And) when he stands, he should have the sword in his left hand, leaning on it, in every town conquered by force, to show them that it was conquered by the sword: 'If you turn back from Islam, then that remains in the hands of the Muslims, they will fight you with it until you return to Islam.' (And) it is sunnah to face the people with his face, just as the Companions faced the Prophet, peace be upon him. (And) it is sunnah (to begin with praise of Allah after seeking refuge silently within himself, and extolling Him as He deserves, and the two testimonies, and sending blessings upon the Prophet, peace be upon him, and admonition by forbidding sins, and frightening and warning against that which incurs the wrath of Allah the Exalted and His punishment, and reminding of that by which there is salvation, and reciting a verse from the Quran), because it is narrated that he recited: '...'
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage from the Hashiya discusses the rulings and sunan of the Friday khutbah. It begins by stating that mere supplications and tasbih are not considered a khutbah, citing the Quranic verse 'So hasten to the remembrance of Allah' (62:9). The term 'remembrance' is general, so any long remembrance qualifies as a khutbah. The proof is also from the incident of Uthman: when he faltered after saying 'Alhamdulillah', he descended and prayed without anyone objecting, indicating consensus that a short khutbah is sufficient. The text then enumerates the sunan of the khutbah, both related to the preacher (khatib) and the khutbah itself, listing eighteen items but noting more can be added. These include: the khatib sitting in his chamber to the right of the minbar wearing black or white; being in a state of purity (taharah) during the khutbah, because it is not prayer. The text clarifies that although an effect mentions it is like half of prayer in reward, the correct view is that purity is not a condition. Covering the private parts is sunnah by inheritance (from the practice of the Companions). Sitting on the minbar before the khutbah and the adhan before it are also inherited practices, as is the iqamah after the khutbah. Standing for both khutbahs is sunnah; sitting is permissible but disliked without excuse. Even delivering the khutbah lying down is valid. When standing, the khatib should hold a sword in his left hand and lean on it in towns conquered by force, as a symbol that the conquest was by the sword and a warning against apostasy. In towns conquered by treaty, no sword is used. Madinah was conquered by the Quran, so no sword; Makkah was by the sword (one of two opinions). Facing the people is sunnah, following the Companions' practice. The content of the khutbah should begin with praise of Allah, the two testimonies, blessings on the Prophet, admonition, and recitation of a Quranic verse, as narrated from the Prophet. The passage thus provides a comprehensive guide to the sunan acts in the Friday sermon.

Key Terms
خطبة (khutbah): The Friday sermon, consisting of two parts with specific sunan content.
ذكر (dhikr): Remembrance of Allah, including praise, supplication, or Quran recitation.
طهارة (taharah): Ritual purity from hadath (minor/major impurity).
سنة (sunnah): Recommended act; omission is valid but disliked without excuse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The khutbah is valid but disliked.
C) The khutbah is valid and not disliked.
D) The khutbah is invalid unless the khatib repeats it.

Key Takeaway
The Friday khutbah is valid with any lengthy dhikr, and its numerous sunan enhance the act without affecting its validity.

Sunan of the Friday Khutbah: Covering, Posture, Sword, and Opening
Explains sunan and rulings of the Friday khutbah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunan of the Friday khutbah according to the Hanafi school and distinguish which actions are recommended versus obligatory.

Core Idea
The Friday khutbah shares reward with prayer, but its sunan—covering 'awrah, sitting, holding a sword in conquered towns, and facing the people—are transmitted practices (tawāruth), not obligations.

This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya on Maraqi al-Falah details the established sunan (recommended acts) of the Friday khutbah. It clarifies that while the khutbah carries meritorious reward akin to prayer, its components include specific postures, symbols, and recitations that are sunnah muakkadah. Understanding these sunan helps in performing the khutbah in the optimal manner transmitted from the early generations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall ruling on the khutbah's reward
The passage begins by stating that the khutbah is 'in the ruling of reward like a part of prayer,' highlighting its merit, but this does not make its components obligatory.
The khutbah is not a pillar (rukn) of Jumu'ah prayer; it is a separate sunnah muakkadah.
2
List the sunan related to posture and covering
Covering the 'awrah during the khutbah is sunnah, not a condition (shart). Sitting on the minbar before beginning, standing for the two khutbahs, and facing the congregation are sunan. Sitting or lying down is valid but disliked without excuse.
The text notes that these are established by transmission (tawāruth), indicating strong practice.
3
Examine the practice of holding a sword
In towns conquered by force (ʿanwatan), the imam holds a sword in his left hand while leaning on it, symbolizing that the town was opened by the sword. In towns conquered by peace (ṣulḥan), no sword is used. Madinah is considered a peace-town (conquered by the Quran); Makkah is an exception as it was conquered by force.
The sword is a symbolic act of sunnah, not wajib.
4
Describe the recommended opening of the khutbah
The imam should begin with silent taʿawwudh (seeking refuge), then praise Allah, send salutations on the Prophet, give admonition by warning against sins, remind of salvation, and recite a verse from the Quran.
The exact Quranic verse is not specified in this excerpt, but it is a sunnah to include an ayah.
5
Note the basis of transmission (tawāruth)
Many of these practices are justified by the fact that they have been transmitted from the early generations (tawāruth), giving them weight as sunan muakkadah. This concept is similar to the Maliki 'amal ahl al-madina.
Tawāruth does not make an act fard; it confirms its recommended status.

Commentary
This passage, from the hashiya of al-Tahtawi on Maraqi al-Falah, delineates many sunan and rulings of the Friday khutbah. It begins by clarifying that the khutbah is like a part of prayer in terms of reward, though it has its own rules. Covering the 'awrah during the khutbah is a sunnah, not a condition, and this is the relied-upon position. The text then addresses the sunan of the khutbah: sitting on the minbar before starting, the adhan being called before the khutbah, and the iqamah after. The imam must stand for the two khutbahs; sitting in both or one is disliked but valid. Lying down is also valid but contrary to sunnah. Regarding the sword: in cities conquered by force, the imam leans on a sword to signify that Islam was established by the sword. In peace-time conquests, no sword is used, except for Makkah which was conquered by force. It is sunnah to face the congregation directly, as the Companions faced the Prophet. The recommended opening includes silent ta'awwudh, praising Allah, the two shahadahs, sending salawat on the Prophet, admonition, and reciting an ayah of the Qur'an. The specific ayah mentioned in the matn is incomplete in the extract.

Key Terms
الخطبة (al-khutbah): The Friday sermon delivered by the imam before the prayer.
التوارث (al-tawāruth): Transmitted practice from the early generations, giving legal weight.
ستر العورة (satr al-'awrah): Covering the private parts during the khutbah; here a sunnah, not a condition.

B) It is valid (sahih) but disliked (makruh) without excuse.
C) It is invalid unless he sits up for parts of it.
D) It is valid and recommended as a sign of humility.

Key Takeaway
The Friday khutbah has many sunan muakkadah, but omitting them does not invalidate the khutbah; they are recommended for perfection.

The Two Khutbahs and Their Recommended Practices
Recommended acts for the two Friday khutbahs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunan of the Friday khutbah and apply the ruling that shortening the khutbah is recommended.

Core Idea
Two short khutbahs with a light sitting are recommended; brevity is a sign of fiqh.

The Friday khutbah is a central act of worship. While its obligation is settled, the Hanafi school details many sunan that elevate its performance. This lesson walks through the two-khutbah format, the content of the second, and the emphasis on conciseness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sunnah of two khutbahs
Recognise that the text states 'sunna khutbatan' — two separate khutbahs, not one, with a sitting between them. This is the established practice transmitted to our time.
Sitting between is also sunnah, lightly (approx. time to recite three verses).
2
List the sunan of the second khutbah
The second khutbah begins with repeating praise, thana, and salawat. It also includes supplication for believers and seeking forgiveness, not admonition (which belongs to the first).
Mentioning the rightful caliphs and uncles is commendable (mustahsan).
3
Recognise voice and brevity recommendations
The imam should raise his voice more in the second khutbah. Both khutbahs should be short — to the extent of a surah from the long mufassal (e.g., Surah al-Hujurat), but less is fine.
Ibn Mas'ud: 'Length of prayer and shortness of khutbah indicate fiqh.'
4
Apply the ruling: sunnah muakkadah
All these acts are sunnah muakkadah, not fard. Omission is makruh (disliked) but does not invalidate the Friday prayer.
Disliked: prolonging the khutbah (especially in summer due to heat/crowding).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و سن خطبتان للتوارث إلى وقتنا (و) سن الجلوس بين الخطبتين جلسة خفيفة وظاهر الرواية مقدار ثلاث آيات (و) سن إعادة الحمد وإعادة الثناء وإعادة الصلاة على النبي كائنة تلك الإعادة في ابتداء الخطبة الثانية وذكر الخلفاء الراشدين والعمين مستحسن بذلك جرى التوارث (و) سن الدعاء فيها أي الخطبة الثانية للمؤمنين والمؤمنات مكان الوعظ بالاستغفار لهم الباء بمعنى مع أي يدعو لهم بإجراء النعم، ودفع النقم، والنصر على الأعداء والمعافاة من الأمراض والادواء مع الاستغفار و (أن يسن بسمع القوم الخطبة ويجهر في الثانية دون الأولى، وإن لم يسمع أجزأ كما في الدراية (و) يسن تخفيف الخطبتين قال ابن مسعود رضي الله عنه : طول من الصلاة، وقصر الخطبة فقه الرجل (بقدر سورة من طوال المفصل) كذا في معراج الدراية، ولكن يراعي الحال بما هو دون ذلك فإنه إذا جاء بذكر وإن قل يكون خطبة (ويكره التطويل) من غير قيد بزمن في الشتاء لقصر الزمان وفي الصيف للضرر بالزحام والحر وترك شيء من السنن التي بيناها
And two khutbahs are recommended by transmission to our time. And sitting between the two khutbahs, a light sitting, is recommended, and the apparent narration is the amount of three verses. And it is recommended to repeat the praise, and repeating the praise, and repeating the prayer upon the Prophet such that that repetition is at the beginning of the second khutbah. And mentioning the rightly-guided caliphs and uncles is commendable; by that transmission proceeded. And supplication in it, meaning the second khutbah, for believing men and women in place of admonition with seeking forgiveness for them. The ba is in the sense of 'with', meaning he supplicates for them for bestowal of blessings, repulsion of calamities, victory over enemies, recovery from illnesses and ailments, along with seeking forgiveness. And that it is recommended that the people hear the khutbah, and he raises his voice in the second but not the first, and if they do not hear, it suffices as in al-Dirayah. And it is recommended to shorten the two khutbahs. Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'The length of the prayer and the shortness of the khutbah indicate the scholar's understanding.' To the extent of a surah from the long mufassal. As in Mi'raj al-Dirayah, but one should consider the situation with something less than that, because if he brings mention even if little, it is a khutbah. And prolonging is disliked without a time restriction in winter due to the short time, and in summer due to the harm of crowding and heat. And omitting any of the established sunan is disliked.
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This section continues the explanation of the khutbah sunan, specifically addressing the format of two khutbahs. It is sunnah to deliver two separate khutbahs with a brief sitting between them, approximately the time to recite three verses. The Prophet initially gave one khutbah but later adopted two for the congregational setting. At the start of the second khutbah, the imam repeats the hamd, thana, and salawat. Mentioning the righteous caliphs and uncles is commendable, though not obligatory. The second khutbah should include supplication for all believers, including forgiveness, well-being, and victory, rather than admonition (which was in the first). It is recommended that the congregation hear the khutbah; if they do not, it still suffices. The khutbah should be concise: as long as one surah from the long mufassal (like Surah al-Hujurat), but shorter is fine. Length is disliked, especially when it causes hardship, and omitting any sunnah of the khutbah is also disliked.

Key Terms
الخطبتان (al-khutbatān): The two separate sermons delivered on Friday, with a sitting between them.
الجهر في الثانية (al-jahr fī al-thāniyah): Raising the voice in the second khutbah more than the first.
التخفيف (al-takhfīf): Shortening the khutbah, considered commendable.

Key Takeaway

Ruling on Walking to Friday Prayer (Sa'y)
Clarifies the obligation and proper manner of proceeding to Friday prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sa'y to Friday prayer and distinguish the proper manner (calm walking) from prohibited brisk walking.

Core Idea
Sa'y to Friday prayer is fard, but it must be done with calmness, not haste.

This passage tackles the obligation of proceeding to Friday prayer. It clarifies that the Quranic command 'fas'aw' does not mean rushing, but walking with dignity. The Prophet himself forbade hurrying, as it removes the believer's dignity. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its evidence, teaching you the correct way to approach the Friday prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
The text begins with 'ويجب يعني يفترض السعي', which explicitly states that sa'y is obligatory (fard). Note that the author equates 'yajib' with 'yufarid', emphasizing the binding nature.
This is the core hukm: fard, not just sunnah.
2
Define the term 'sa'y' in context
The author explains that sa'y here means 'going walking with calmness and dignity' (الذهاب ماشياً بالسكينة والوقار). This is the legally prescribed manner.
The word 'sa'y' can mean exertion, but in this verse it is interpreted as calm walking.
3
Contrast with prohibited behavior
The text explicitly prohibits 'harwalah' (brisk walking) because it 'takes away the dignity of the believer' (تذهب بهاء المؤمن). The Prophet is quoted as forbidding it.
This makes harwalah at least makruh tahrimi, if not haram, in this context.
4
Understand the wisdom and exceptions
Walking is better for those who are able; the phrase 'لمن يقدر عليه' implies that those unable may use alternative means. The word 'sa'y' is used to match the Quranic command, not to imply rushing.
The manner is tied to ability; the obligation of attendance remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And it is obligatory, meaning fard, to sa'y. He intended going walking with calmness and dignity, not brisk walking, because it takes away the beauty of the believer. And walking is better for one who is able, and also when returning from it. Commentary
This passage addresses the obligation of attending the Friday prayer. The term 'sa'y' is used in the Qur'an (u201cYaa ayyuha alladhina aamanu idha nudiya lilssalati min yawmi al-jumu'ati fas'aw ila dhikrillahi u2013 u201cO you who believe, when the call is made for prayer on Friday, proceed to the remembrance of Allahu201d). The author clarifies that 'sa'y' here means to go walking with calmness and dignity, not to run or hurry. The Prophet forbade rushing, as it reduces one's dignity. Walking is better than riding for those who are able. The ruling: it is fard to go, but the manner is calm. This is consistent with the hadith: u201cIf you come to the prayer, come with calmness and dignity, and do not rush.u201d

Key Terms
السعي (al-sa'y): Going to the Friday prayer; here interpreted as walking calmly.
الهرولة (al-harwalah): Brisk walking or hurrying; prohibited for Friday prayer as it diminishes one's dignity.

Key Takeaway

Sunan of the Two Friday Sermons
Lists recommended acts in the Friday khutbah and discusses their rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunan (recommended acts) of the Friday khutbah according to the Hanafi school and identify which of these are subject to scholarly difference.

Core Idea
The Friday sermons are sunnah muakkadah; each recommended act enhances the khutbah.

The Friday prayer is a weekly congregational obligation, preceded by two sermons. Beyond basic validity, there are numerous sunan that elevate the khutbah. This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi enumerates these sunan and clarifies their legal status within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
List each recommended act mentioned: two sermons, sitting between them, repeating praise and salawat, mentioning caliphs, supplication, being heard, and shortening. Note that all are classified as sunnah.
Some acts have additional details, e.g., sitting measure of three verses.
2
Distinguish sunnah from wajib
Understand that leaving a sunnah is not sinful but contrary to the ideal. The text uses 'سن' (sunnah) explicitly.
Contrast with fard or wajib acts that require repetition.
3
Note the majority view and apparent narration
For seeking refuge, the majority ('الأكثر') hold that it is before the sermon. For sitting, the apparent narration (ظاهر الرواية) specifies three verses.
These are not disputed within the Hanafi school.
4
Analyze the application of shortening
Shortening is sunnah; ideal length is a long mufassal surah, but less is acceptable. Lengthening is makruh, especially in winter (short days) and summer (heat/crowd).
The hadith of Ibn Mas'ud supports shortening.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلاة
في خطبته واتقوا يوماً ترجعون فيه إلى الله والأكثر على أنه يتعوّذ قبلها، ولا يسمي إلا أن يقرأ سورة كامله فيسمي أيضاً (و) سن (خطبتان) للتوارث إلى وقتنا (و) سن (الجلوس بين الخطبتين) جلسة خفيفة وظاهر الرواية مقدار ثلاث آيات (و) سن (إعادة الحمد و) إعادة (الثناء و) إعادة (الصلاة) على النبي ( كائنة تلك الإعادة (في ابتداء الخطبة الثانية) وذكر الخلفاء الراشدين والعمين مستحسن بذلك جرى التوارث (و) سن (الدعاء فيها) أي الخطبة الثانية (للمؤمنين والمؤمنات مكان الوعظ (بالاستغفار لهم الباء بمعنى مع أي يدعو لهم بإجراء النعم، ودفع النقم، والنصر على الأعداء والمعافاة من الأمراض والادواء مع (الاستغفار و) (أن يسن بسمع القوم الخطبة ويجهر في الثانية دون الأولى، وإن لم يسمع أجزأ كما في الدراية (و) يسن (تخفيف الخطبتين قال ابن مسعود رضي الله عنه : طول الصلاة، وقصر الخطبة فقه الرجل (بقدر سورة من طوال المفصل) كذا في معراج الدراية، ولكن يراعي الحال بما هو دون ذلك فإنه إذا جاء بذكر وإن قل يكون خطبة (ويكره التطويل) من غير قيد بزمن في الشتاء لقصر الزمان وفي الصيف للضرر بالزحام والحر وترك شيء من السنن التي بيناها
Book of Prayer. Regarding his sermon: 'And fear a day when you will be returned to Allah.' The majority hold that he seeks refuge before it, and he does not say the basmalah unless he recites a complete surah, so he also says the basmalah. (And) two sermons are sunnah, according to transmitted practice up to our time. (And) sitting between the two sermons is sunnah, a light sitting, and according to the apparent narration, the measure of three verses. (And) it is sunnah to repeat the praise, and to repeat the glorification, and to repeat the prayer upon the Prophet, that repetition being at the beginning of the second sermon. Mentioning the Rightly Guided Caliphs and the two uncles is considered good, and so it has been transmitted. (And) supplication in it (i.e., the second sermon) for the believing men and women is sunnah in place of admonition, asking forgiveness for them; 'with' meaning 'together with' i.e., he supplicates for them for the granting of blessings, repelling of afflictions, victory over enemies, recovery from illnesses and ailments, along with seeking forgiveness. (And) that the people should hear the sermon is sunnah, and he raises his voice in the second [sermon] but not in the first; if they do not hear, it is sufficient, as in al-Diraya. (And) it is sunnah to lighten the two sermons. Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'Length of prayer and shortness of the sermon is understanding of the man' (to the extent of a surah from the long mufassal). Thus in Mi'raj al-Diraya, but one should consider the situation with what is less than that; for if he comes up with remembrance even if little, it becomes a sermon. (And) lengthening is disliked, without restriction to a season: in winter because of the shortness of the day, and in summer because of harm from crowding and heat; and leaving any of the sunan we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi outlines the sunan (recommended acts) of the two Friday sermons. The text states the positions: seeking refuge before the sermon, reciting basmalah only if reciting a complete surah, the sunna status of having two sermons with a light sitting between them (measure of three verses according to the apparent narration). It is sunna to repeat praise, glorification, and salawat at the beginning of the second sermon, and to mention the Rightly Guided Caliphs and the Prophet's uncles (considered good). In the second sermon, supplication for believers including forgiveness, blessings, victory, and health replaces admonition. The imam should be heard; he raises his voice in the second sermon. Shortening the sermons is sunna, with an ideal length of a long mufassal surah, but less is acceptable. Lengthening is disliked (makruh) especially in winter due to short days and in summer due to heat and crowding.

Key Terms
خطبتان (khutbatān): The two sermons delivered during the Friday congregational prayer, sitting between them is sunnah.
الجلوس بين الخطبتين (al-julūs bayn al-khuṭbatayn): A light sitting between the two Friday sermons, of approximately three verses' duration.
تخفيف الخطبتين (takhfīf al-khuṭbatayn): Shortening the two sermons to a length equivalent to a long surah of the mufassal (Quran section), or even less if the congregation needs it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Lengthening the sermons

C) Sitting between the sermons

Key Takeaway
The two Friday sermons and their associated acts are sunnah muakkadah; shortening the sermon is recommended while lengthening is disliked.

Obligation of Sa'y and Walking Calmly to Friday Prayer
Clarifies the obligation of hastening to Friday prayer while emphasizing calm walking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling regarding sa'y for Friday prayer and state the correct demeanor prescribed by the hadith.

Core Idea
Sa'y for Friday prayer is fard, but it means walking calmly, not running.

This passage clarifies a crucial distinction: the Quranic command 'fas'aw ila dhikrillah' does not command physical running. The Hanafi school, guided by the Prophet's ﷺ explicit prohibition of rushing to prayer, interprets sa'y as walking with dignity and tranquility. Understanding this removes confusion and aligns practice with the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act under discussion is sa'y (going to Friday prayer). According to the Hanafi school, it is fard (obligatory) upon those who meet the conditions of Jumu'ah.
The word 'wajib' in the text is equated with 'fard' here.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites a hadith from Ahmad: 'When the prayer has been established, do not come running; come walking with tranquility.'
This hadith is ahad but its meaning is clear (dalala qati).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith explicitly prohibits running (tas'u) and commands walking with tranquility (tamshuna wa 'alaykum al-sakina). This shows that the required manner for attending prayer is calm walking, not rushing.
The imperative 'أتوها تمشون' indicates the prescribed mode.
4
Note the potential misconception
The literal meaning of 'sa'y' in Arabic includes 'hastening' or 'running', which could lead to misunderstanding. The author addresses this by linking the term to the Quranic verse but clarifying the actual practice.
The refinement is critical: sa'y is fard but the manner is constrained by the Sunnah.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is based on the explicit text of the hadith, which overrides any literal interpretation of the word. Walking calmly is the operative ruling (mufta bihi).
No intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is mentioned; all three imams agree on this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب يعني يفترض السعي أراد الذهاب ماشياً بالسكينة والوقار لا الهرولة لأنها تذهب بهاء المؤمن والمشي أفضل لمن يقدر عليه وفي العود منها وإنما ذكر بلفظ السعي لمطابقة الأمر به في الآية وقد نهى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عنه بقوله : إذا أقيمت الصلاة فلا تأتوها وأنتم تسعون وأتوها تمشون وعليكم السكينة، فما أدركتم فصلوا وما فاتكم فأتموا . وأخرجه أحمد، وقال: وما فاتكم فامضوا فيذهب في الأولى وهو الأفضل، ثم ما يليها وهكذا للجمعة
And it is obligatory meaning it is fard: the sa'y (hastening) – he intended going walking with tranquility and dignity, not hurried running because it takes away the dignity of the believer. Walking is better for one who is able, both in going and returning. It is only mentioned with the word sa'y to match the commandment in the verse. The Prophet ﷺ prohibited rushing by saying: 'When the prayer has been established, do not come to it while you are running; come walking with tranquility. Whatever you catch, pray, and whatever you miss, complete.' Ahmad narrated it, and he said: 'And whatever you miss, complete.' So one goes in the first hour – that is the best, then the next, and so on for Friday.
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage explains the obligation of 'sa'y' (hastening) to the Friday prayer. The author clarifies that sa'y here does not mean physically running but rather walking with calmness and dignity, as rushing detracts from the believer's decorum. The word 'sa'y' is used because it corresponds to the Quranic command 'فاسعوا إلى ذكر الله' (So hasten to the remembrance of Allah). The Prophet ﷺ explicitly prohibited running to prayer in the hadith reported by Ahmad. The text also encourages arriving early, with the first hour being the best, followed by subsequent hours. This applies to the Friday prayer.

Key Terms
السعي (al-sa'y): The obligation to go to Friday prayer, but interpreted as walking with dignity and tranquility, not rushing.
السكينة والوقار (al-sakīna wa al-waqār): Tranquility and dignity – the proper demeanor when proceeding to prayer.

The Etiquette of Walking to Friday Prayer: Prohibition of Haste and Virtue of Going Early
Explaining the hadith on not running to prayer and the rewards of going early to Jumu'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on walking to Friday prayer and explain the evidence for the prohibition of haste and the reward for going early.

Core Idea
The Prophet commanded walking with dignity, not running; going early brings immense rewards, like sacrificing a camel.

Friday prayer carries special etiquettes. The Prophet explicitly forbade rushing and commanded calmness. In this passage, al-Tahtawi explains the hadith and the virtues of arriving early, showing that haste is not only disliked but prohibited (makruh tahrimi), while going early is a highly rewarded sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act of walking to Friday prayer: walking calmly is sunnah muakkadah; running is makruh tahrimi. Going early (tabkir) is mustahabb.
The hadith explicitly commands 'come walking' and negates running.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith of Abu Hurairah (quoted via Ahmad and Abu Dawud): 'When the prayer is established, do not come running, but come walking and have tranquility.'
This hadith is mutawatir in meaning (qat'i al-thubut).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'أتوها' (come) combined with the negation of running indicates a command to walk calmly. The phrase 'وعليكم السكينة' (upon you be tranquility) emphasizes dignity.
In Hanafi usul, al-amr yufidu al-wujub, but here the context shows it is a guidance (sunnah) not obligation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is disagreement on the interpretation of the five hours mentioned in Malik's hadith: some (Malik, Imam al-Haramayn) say they are brief moments after zawal; the majority say they are ordinary hours of the day.
The Hanafi school follows the majority view that the hours are the regular 24-hour parts, encouraging early arrival from sunrise.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon position is that going early is mustahabb, and the best time to go is from sunrise (per al-Burhan al-Halabi). Rushing is makruh tahrimi.
The reward for going early is immense - for every step, a year's fasting and prayer.

إذا أقيمت الصلاة فلا تأتوها وأنتم تسعون وأتوها تمشون وعليكم السكينة، فما أدركتم فصلوا وما فاتكم فأتموا . وأخرجه أحمد، وقال: وما فاتكم فامضوا فيذهب في الأولى وهو الأفضل، ثم ما يليها وهكذا للجمعة (و) يجب بمعنى يفترض (ترك البيع) وكذا من الساعة قوله: (وأنتم تسعون أي تسرعون قوله : (وقال) أي الإمام أحمد ومثله عند ابن حبان عن ابن عيينة . قوله : فيذهب في الساعة الأولى الخ) لحديث أوس الثقفي رضي الله عنه من غسل يوم الجمعة واغتسل ثم بكر وابتكر ومشى ، ولم يركب ودنا من الإمام واستمع ولم يلغ كان له بكل خطوة عمل سنة أجر صيامها وقيامها رواه أبو داود، وغيره يقال : غسل الرجل امرأته، وغسلها مخففاً، ومشدداً إذا جامعها لأنه أوجب عليها الغسل بجماعه، وورد أن فعله كان ممن يظل بظل العرش كذا ذكره الشبرخيتي في شرح الأربعين، والتبكير سرعة الانتباه أول الوقت أو قبله لأداء العبادة بنشاط والابتكار هو المسارعة إلى المصلى لينال فضيلته، والصف الأول، وروى الإمام مالك في الموطأ قال : من اغتسل يوم الجمعة غسل الجنابة، ثم راح في الساعة الأولى فكأنما قرب بدنة، ومن راح في الساعة الثانية فكأنما قرب بقرة ومن راح في الثالثة فكأنما قرب كبشاً أقرن ومن راح في الساعة الرابعة فكأنما قرب دجاجة، ومن راح في الساعة الخامسة فكأنما قرب بيضة .اهـ. قال مالك وأكثر أصحابه وإمام الحرمين، والقاضي حسين أنها لحظات لطيفة أولها زوال الشمس، وآخرها قعود الإمام على المنبر وقال الجمهور والمراد ساعات اليوم والليلة المنقسمة إلى أربعة وعشرين جزءاً فاستحبوا التبكير اليها واختلف في أول الوقت فقيل : من طلوع الشمس ليكون ما قبله من طلوع الفجر زمان غسل، وتأهب . قال البرهان الحلبي : وهو الأظهر وذكر الساعات للحث على التكبير إليها، والترغيب في فضلة السبق وتحصيل الصف الأول، وانتظارها، والاشتغال بالنفل، والذكر قبلها، وفي الكشاف قيل : أول بدعة حدثت في الإسلام ترك البكور إلى الجمعة، ومعنى راح في الحديث خف قال في القاموس : راح للمعروف يراح راحة أخذته له خفة، وراحت يده لكذا ،خفت واستحبوا ان يواقع زوجته ليكون أغضى لبصره، وأسكن لنفسه إذا راح للجمعة كما يشهد له حديث أوس السابق.
When the prayer is established, do not come to it running, but come walking and have tranquility. Whatever you catch, pray, and whatever you miss, complete. Ahmad narrated it, and he said: 'And what you miss, proceed' – he goes in the first hour and that is best, then the next, and so on for Friday. (And) it is obligatory in the sense of fard to leave sale, and likewise from the hour. His statement: (and you are running) i.e., hurrying. His statement: (and he said) i.e., Imam Ahmad, and similar is with Ibn Hibban from Ibn Uyaynah. His statement: (he goes in the first hour) etc. for the hadith of Aws al-Thaqafi, may Allah be pleased with him: 'Whoever washes on Friday and then goes early and rides not, and draws near the Imam and listens and does not talk, will have for every step the reward of a year's fasting and prayer.' Abu Dawud narrated it. It is said: 'ghassala' and 'ghassala' lightly and heavily if he had intercourse with her, because he obliged her to bathe. It is reported that his action was among those shaded under the Throne. Al-Shabramallisi mentioned it. Al-Tabkir is hastening to attention at the beginning or before. Al-Ibtikar is rushing to the prayer place to gain virtue and the first row. Imam Malik narrated in al-Muwatta: 'Whoever bathes on Friday with the bath of janabah, then goes in the first hour is as if he sacrificed a camel; in the second hour a cow; in the third a horned ram; in the fourth a chicken; in the fifth an egg.' Malik and most of his companions, Imam al-Haramayn, and Qadi Husayn said they are subtle moments starting from zawal and ending when the Imam sits on the minbar. The majority said they are hours of the day and night divided into 24 parts. They recommended going early. There is disagreement on the beginning time: some said from sunrise so that the time from fajr is for washing and preparation. Al-Burhan al-Halabi said it is the most apparent. The mention of hours is to encourage going early and competing in virtue and obtaining the first row, waiting, and engaging in supererogatory prayer and dhikr before. In al-Kashshaf it is said: The first innovation in Islam was leaving early for Friday. The meaning of 'raha' in the hadith is lightness. In al-Qamus: 'raha lil-ma'ruf yarahu raha' he took lightness. His hand became light for it. They recommended that he have intercourse with his wife so that his gaze might be lowered and his soul more tranquil when he goes for Friday, as the hadith of Aws indicates.
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof about the etiquette of going to Friday prayer. It begins with the famous hadith of the Prophet (saw) prohibiting running to prayer and commanding tranquility. The commentator explains that the phrase 'do not come running' means do not hurry. The hadith is also narrated by Ahmad with a slight variation. The passage then discusses the virtue of going early, citing a hadith of Aws al-Thaqafi about the reward of steps. It defines 'tabkir' as going early and 'ibtikar' as rushing to the mosque. Imam Malik's Muwatta is quoted to show the five hours of Friday and their respective rewards, likening them to offering a camel, cow, ram, chicken, and egg. The commentator notes that some scholars (Malik, Imam al-Haramayn) interpret these as brief moments close to the sermon, while the majority view them as parts of the day. The preferred view is that the time begins at sunrise. The passage concludes with a recommendation for marital intercourse before going, as it helps lower the gaze and calm the soul. The overall message is to go early and calmly for greater spiritual benefit.

Key Terms
التبكير (al-tabkir): Going early to the Friday prayer at the beginning of the time or before.
الساعة (al-sa'ah): An hour of the day, interpreted by some as a brief moment after zawal and by others as a regular hour of the 24-hour day.
السكينة (al-sakinah): Tranquility and dignity in walking, not rushing.

The Friday Adhan Prohibition: Valid Sale or Void?
Ruling with proof on Friday adhan prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on transactions after the Friday adhan and explain why the contract remains valid despite the prohibition.

Core Idea
A sale after Friday adhan is sinful but valid – the prohibition targets the act, not the contract.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on buying and selling after the first call to Friday prayer. You will learn the distinction between sin and contract validity, the time the prohibition begins, and how it differs from other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is engaging in sale or purchase after the first Friday adhan. The Hanafi ruling is makruh tahrimi – sinful but the contract is valid.
The term 'haram' is sometimes used interchangeably, but the technical term is makruh tahrimi.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the Quranic command 'leave trade' (Quran 62:9). In Hanafi usul, a prohibition in amr form indicates sin but does not automatically void the contract unless explicitly stated.
3
Distinguish sin from validity
In Hanafi fiqh, the prohibition is on the act of transacting, not on the contract's formation. Since the contract meets all conditions of validity, it remains sahih. The sin is incurred by the act itself.
This is a key difference from schools that treat the prohibition as implying nullity.
4
Analyze the inter-madhhab ikhtilaf
Malik and Ahmad hold the contract is batil (void) except for marriage, gift, and charity. Their dalil: a clear prohibition must invalidate the act. The Hanafi response: the prohibition is on the external act, not the internal contract pillars.
This ikhtilaf produces a practical difference in ownership transfer.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical effect
The relied-upon position is that the sale is valid; the buyer owns the item before possession and the price is due. The first adhan (after zawal) is the trigger, not the second adhan at the minbar.
Intra-Hanafi: al-Tahawi favored the second adhan, but Shams al-A'imma's view is preferred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ويجب ترك البيع) فيكره تحريماً من الطرفين على المذهب، وصح اطلاق الحرام عليه كما وقع في الهداية، ويقع العقد صحيحاً عندنا، وهو قول الجمهور حتى يجب الثمن، ويثبت الملك قبل القبض، وفي الفتح المكروه دون الفاسد، وليس المراد بكونه دونه في حكم المنع الشرعي، بل في عدم فساد العقد، وإلا فهذه المكروهات كلها تحريمية لا نعلم خلافاً في الإثم بها اهـ وقال مالك وأحمد: بالبطلان في غير نكاح، وهبة ،وصدقة وفي الكلام إشعار بأن من لم تجب عليه الجمعة مستثنى من الحكم كما في القهستاني يعني من لم تجب عليهما معاً أما إذا وجبت على أحدهما دون الآخر أثما جميعاً لأن الأول ارتكب النهي والثاني أعانه عليه كذا في شرح البخاري للعيني. قوله : (وكذا ترك كل شيء يؤدي إلى الاشتغال عن السعي إليها أو يخل به كالبيع ماشياً إليها لا طلاق الأمر (بالأذان (الأول) الواقع بعد الزوال (في الأصح لحصول الإعلام به لأنه لو انتظر الأذان الثاني الذي عند المنبر تفوته السنة وربما لا يدرك الجمعة لبعد محله وهو اختيار شمس الأئمة قوله: (في (الأصح) وقال الطحاوي: المعتبر هو الأذان الثاني عند المنبر لأنه الذي كان في زمنه ، والشيخين بعده قال في البحر وهو ضعيف.
His statement: (And leaving sale is obligatory) meaning it is makruh tahrimi from both sides in the madhhab, and it is correctly called haram as in al-Hidayah. The contract is valid according to us, and it is the majority view; so the price is due and ownership is established before possession. In al-Fath, the sale is makruh not fasid. The intent is not that it is less in prohibition, but that the contract is not nullified; otherwise, these all are makruh tahrimi with sin agreed upon. Malik and Ahmad said it is batil except for marriage, gift, and charity. The speech indicates that one not obliged to Friday is exempted, as in al-Qahistani. That is, one not obliged to both? But if one is obliged and the other not, both sin because one commits the prohibition and the other assists. Al-Ayni said that in his Sharh al-Bukhari. His statement: (And likewise leaving everything that distracts from going or harms it, like selling while walking to it, not divorce. The matter is by the first adhan after zawal, in the most correct view, because it gives notice; if he waited for the second adhan at the minbar, he would miss the sunnah and possibly miss Friday due to distance. This is the choice of Shams al-A'imma. His statement: (in the most correct) And al-Tahawi said: The valid one is the second adhan at the minbar because that was in his time and the two shaykhs. Al-Bahr said it is weak.
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof about the prohibition of buying, selling, and other transactions after the first adhan on Friday. The commentator begins by clarifying that the ruling is makruh tahrimi in the Hanafi school, and some even call it haram. Importantly, the contract remains valid (sahih) and not void (batil), which is the Hanafi position as opposed to Malik and Ahmad who consider it batil except for marriage, gift, and charity. The sin applies to both buyer and seller if both are obligated to attend Friday prayer. The prohibition extends to any activity that distracts from going, such as selling while walking (though walking itself is not prohibited). The time of prohibition is from the first adhan after zawal, not the second adhan at the minbar, because the first adhan gives sufficient notice. There is an intra-Hanafi difference: Shams al-A'imma and the majority favor the first adhan, while al-Tahawi considered the second. The relied-upon view is the first adhan.

Key Terms
الكراهة التحريمية (al-karahah al-tahrimiyyah): A prohibited action with sin, but the contract remains valid.
الأذان الأول (al-adhan al-awwal): The first call to Friday prayer after zawal, which begins the prohibition of trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The sale is valid but sinful.
C) The sale is valid and recommended.
D) The sale is invalid but not sinful.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a sale after the Friday adhan is sinful but valid – the prohibition targets the act of transacting, not the contract itself.

Abandoning Distractions for Jumu'ah and the First Adhan
Commentary on abandoning distractions for Jumu'ah and preference for first adhan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on abandoning distractions while going to Jumu'ah and explain the preference for the first adhan after zawal, citing the underlying istihsan reasoning.

Core Idea
Walking to Jumu'ah means abandoning all distractions – even selling – and the first adhan after zawal is preferred to ensure timely attendance.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi clarifies two interconnected rulings in the Hanafi school: the obligation to abandon anything that distracts from attending Jumu'ah, including buying and selling while walking, and the preference for the first adhan over the second at the minbar. The commentary uses a rational proof (istihsan) based on achieving the purpose of notification and preventing loss of the prayer. It also highlights an intra-madhhab difference regarding which adhan is relied upon.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objects of the ruling
The passage rules on two things: (1) abandoning all distractions while going to Jumu'ah, exemplified by selling while walking; (2) the status of the first adhan after zawal versus the second adhan at the minbar.
2
Determine the legal category for each
For distractions: the obligation to abandon them is wajib; engaging in them (e.g., selling) is haram because it hinders the obligatory sa'y. For the adhan: the first adhan is preferred (sunnah mu'akkadah).
The text explicitly indicates the first adhan is the superior practice.
3
Analyze the proof (dalil)
The proof for preferring the first adhan is istihsan: it rationally achieves the aim of notification and avoids losing the sunnah or the prayer. The proof is rational (aqli), not textual.
Istihsan is a valid Hanafi proof when strict analogy would lead to hardship.
4
Recognize the intra-madhhab difference
Some Hanafis (e.g., al-Tahawi) considered the second adhan at the minbar to be the standard, but the majority (including Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani) choose the first adhan. The author adopts the majority view as mufta bihi.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترك كل شيء يؤدي إلى الاشتغال عن السعي إليها أو يخل به كالبيع ماشياً إليها لا طلاق الأمر (بالأذان (الأول) الواقع بعد الزوال (في الأصح لحصول الإعلام به لأنه لو انتظر الأذان الثاني الذي عند المنبر تفوته السنة وربما لا يدرك الجمعة لبعد محله وهو اختيار شمس الأئمة
Abandoning everything that leads to preoccupation from going to it or disrupts it, like selling while walking to it, and there is no abrogation of the command regarding the first adhan occurring after zawal, according to the most correct view, because the notification is achieved by it; for if one waits for the second adhan at the minbar, he loses the sunnah and perhaps will not catch Jumu'ah due to the distance of his place, and this is the choice of Shams al-A'imma.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the base text's ruling that one must abandon everything that distracts from attending Jumu'ah prayer. The writer explains that this includes economic activities like buying and selling while actually walking to the mosque, which is impermissible. He then addresses a related issue: the adhan. He states that the first adhan after zawal (when the sun declines) is the preferred one in the Hanafi school, not the second adhan at the minbar. The reasoning is that the first adhan effectively announces the time, and if people waited for the second adhan, they might miss the sunnah of the sermon and even the prayer itself. This is the choice of Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani. The passage also implicitly indicates that the command to leave all distractions applies absolutely and is not waived for the adhan.

Key Terms
السعي إلى الجمعة (al-sa'y ila al-jumu'ah): The act of going to the Friday prayer; the text emphasizes abandoning anything that hinders it.
الأذان الأول (al-adhan al-awwal): The first call to prayer for Jumu'ah, given after zawal at the mosque, as opposed to the second adhan at the minbar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The first adhan is louder.
C) It ensures notification and prevents missing the sunnah and possibly the prayer.
D) It is commanded in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Abandoning all distractions when going to Jumu'ah is wajib, and the first adhan after zawal is the preferred practice in the Hanafi school.

Prohibition of Prayer and Speech When the Imam Appears for Jumu'ah
Rulings on prohibition of prayer and speech and listening to khutbah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the prohibition of prayer and speech when the imam appears for Jumu'ah, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute, and apply the rule to actions during the khutbah.

Core Idea
The moment the imam appears, all prayer and speech are prohibited because listening to the khutbah becomes obligatory.

The Friday sermon (khutbah) is a central part of Jumu'ah, and the congregation's conduct during it is strictly regulated. This lesson examines the precise moment when the prohibition of speech and prayer begins, the differences among the early Hanafi imams, and how this obligation affects actions like responding to a sneeze, reading Quran, or writing. Understanding these details ensures that one's Jumu'ah is valid and rewards are preserved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trigger event
Note that the ruling hinges on the imam's appearance (khuruj al-imam), not merely the start of the sermon. The prohibition begins even before the imam ascends the minbar.
This is a key practical point: once the imam walks out, you must stop praying, talking, or any distracting activity.
2
State the base ruling and its evidence
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that when the imam appears, it is forbidden to pray or speak. The evidence is a prophetic text (hadith) that commands silence and attention.
The text does not quote the hadith verbatim but asserts its authority.
3
Compare the Sahibayn's positions
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow speech before the khutbah begins and before the imam says the takbir. They differ on sitting silently when the imam is silent. Understand the reasoning: Abu Yusuf sees no obligation to listen when there is no speech; Muhammad holds that the original prohibition remains.
This intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf has practical consequences for the congregation's behavior.
4
Apply the prohibition to other actions
The passage extends the ruling to actions like responding to a sneeze or greeting, reading Quran, and writing. Some exceptions exist for those far from the imam, as long as they do not disturb others.
The principle is that any act that could distract from obligatory listening is discouraged or forbidden.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا خرج الإمام فلا صلاة ولا كلام وهو قول الإمام لأنه نص النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد لا بأس بالكلام إذا خرج قبل أن يخطب وإذا نزل قبل أن يكبر، واختلفا في جلوسه إذا سكت، فعند أبي يوسف يباح وعند محمد لا يباح لأن الكراهة للإخلال بفرض الاستماع ولا استماع هنا وله إطلاق الأمر، وإذا أمر الخطيب بالصلاة على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم سراً إحرازاً للفضيلتين، ويحمد في نفسه إذا عطس على الصحيح، وفي الينابيع يكره التسبيح، وقراءة القرآن والصلاة على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا كان يسمع وروي عن نصير بن يحيى إن كان بعيداً من الإمام يقرأ القرآن، وروي عنه أنه كان يحرك شفتيه، ويقرأ القرآن فمن فعل مثله، ولا يشغل غيره بسماع تلاوته لا بأس به كالنظر في الكتاب، والكتابة، وفيه خلاف وروي عن أبي يوسف أنه لا بأس به وقال الحسن بن زیاد ما دخل العراق أحد أفقه من الحكم بن زهير وإن الحكم كان يجلس مع أبي يوسف يوم الجمعة، وينظر في كتابه ويصحح بالقلم وقت الخطبة ولا يرد سلاماً ولا يشمت عاطساً) لاشتغاله بسماع واجب قال في الحجة كان أبو حنيفة رحمه الله يكره تشميت العاطس،
And when the imam comes out, there is no prayer and no speech, and this is the statement of Imam (Abu Hanifa) because of the text of the Prophet, peace be upon him. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: there is no harm in speech when he has come out before he preaches, and when he descends before he says the takbir, and they differed concerning his sitting when he is silent: according to Abu Yusuf it is permissible, and according to Muhammad it is not permissible because the dislike is due to violating the obligation of listening, and there is no listening here, and he has the absolute command. And when the preacher commands to send blessings upon the Prophet, peace be upon him, secretly to attain both virtues, and he praises in himself if he sneezes, according to the correct view. In al-Yanabi': it is disliked to do tasbih, recite Quran, and send blessings upon the Prophet if he can hear. And it is narrated from Nasir ibn Yahya that if he is far from the imam, he may recite Quran. And it is narrated from him that he used to move his lips and recite Quran; whoever does similarly and does not distract others with the sound of his recitation, there is no harm, like looking in a book and writing, though there is a difference of opinion. And it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that there is no harm. Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad said: no one more knowledgeable than al-Hakam ibn Zuhair entered Iraq, and al-Hakam used to sit with Abu Yusuf on Friday and look in his book and correct with a pen during the sermon. And he does not respond to a greeting nor does he say yarhamuka Allah to a sneezer, because he is occupied with obligatory listening. Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, used to dislike saying yarhamuka Allah to a sneezer.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement addressing the detailed rulings of conduct during Jumu'ah when the imam appears. The author begins by stating the Hanafi position: once the imam emerges (even before ascending the minbar), all prayer and speech are prohibited. This is the view of Imam Abu Hanifa, based on a prophetic text. The two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, differ: they permit speech before the preaching begins, and before the imam says the takbir. They also differ on sitting silently: Abu Yusuf allows, Muhammad disallows. The reasoning for prohibition is the obligation to listen to the sermon; when the imam is silent, there is no listening, but Muhammad holds that the absolute command (to remain silent) still applies. The commentary then extends to actions during the sermon: when the preacher tells the congregation to send blessings on the Prophet, it should be done silently. One may praise Allah silently for a sneeze. However, tasbih, Quran recitation, and prayers that can be heard are disliked. But if one is far from the imam, some early jurists allowed moving lips without sound or even reciting Quran quietly, as long as it does not disturb others. Looking in a book and writing during the sermon is debated; Abu Yusuf allowed it, and the practice of the scholar al-Hakam ibn Zuhair is cited. The passage concludes with the rule: one must not respond to a verbal greeting or say yarhamuka Allah to a sneezer, because listening to the sermon is obligatory and overrides these acts. Abu Hanifa disliked even saying yarhamuka Allah to a sneezer during the khutbah.

Key Terms
خروج الإمام (khuruj al-imam): The moment the imam emerges from his residence or stands to ascend the minbar; triggers prohibition of prayer and speech.
فرض الاستماع (fard al-istima'): The obligatory act of listening to the Friday sermon; it is considered a fard (obligation) in the context of Jumu'ah.
تشميت العاطس (tashmit al-'atis): Saying 'yarhamuka Allah' (may Allah have mercy on you) to a person who sneezes; during the khutbah, it is forbidden.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on writing with a pen during the Friday sermon according to al-Hakam ibn Zuhair's practice?
A) Forbidden because it distracts from listening
B) Permissible as long as one does not disturb others
C) Encouraged to take notes on the sermon
D) Only permissible for teachers

Key Takeaway
The imam's appearance for Jumu'ah triggers the obligation to remain silent and listen; even apparently minor acts like responding to a sneeze are forbidden.

Rulings on Actions During the Friday Khutbah: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Sitting and Prohibition of Distractions
Intra-Hanafi disputes on actions during the Friday khutbah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on sitting during the silent pause and on responding to sneezes and greetings during the Friday khutbah, and explain the rationale behind the relied-upon view.

Core Idea
Silence is wajib during the khutbah; even responding to a sneeze or greeting is prohibited.

This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi addresses key intra-Hanafi disagreements regarding the imam's posture during the silent pause and the permissibility of various responses. It clarifies that the relied-upon position prohibits all distracting actions, emphasizing the obligation of attentive listening.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed action
Recognize that the dispute is about the imam sitting silently during the pause in the khutbah.
2
State the two Hanafi positions
Abu Yusuf permits sitting; Muhammad al-Shaybani prohibits it.
3
Analyze the underlying reasoning (ta'lil)
Abu Yusuf argues that the prohibition is tied to listening, which is absent during the pause. Muhammad holds that the command to listen is absolute.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
The difference affects whether it is permissible to sit silently during the pause.
5
Conclude with the mufta bihi (relied-upon position)
The author sides with Muhammad, stating that all distracting actions are prohibited.

الولوالجية النسائي عن الخطيب إذا كان يجيب لا الخطبة لا يقرأ القرآن، بل يسمع ويسكت هو المختار. قوله: (وقال الحسن الخ) معتمد المذهب المنع قال في الكنز بل يستمع وينصت والناسي كالقريب. قوله: (وإن الحكم) بكسر إن. قوله: (ولا يرد سلاماً) مطلقاً لا بلسانه ولا بقلبه لا قبل الفراغ ولا بعده لأن هذا السلام غير مأذون فيه شرعاً، بل يرتكب بسلامه إثماً لأنه يشغل به خاطر السامع عن الفرض. قوله: (ولا يشمت عاطساً (وكره) لمن تجب عليه الجمعة (الخروج من المصر يوم الجمعة بعد النداء) أي الأذان الأول وقيل الثاني: (ما لم يصل الجمعة) لأنه شمله الأمر بالسعي قبل تحققه بالسير، وإذا خرج قبل الزوال فلا بأس به بلا خلاف عندنا، وكذا بعد الفراغ منها وإن لم يدركها. (ومن لا جمعة عليه كمريض ومسافر ورقيق وامرأة وعمى ومقعد إن أداها جاز عن فرضه). قوله: (قدمناه من قوله إذا خرج الإمام الخ). قوله: (وليس منه) أي من الكلام المكروه. قوله: (حق الله) بدل من الإنصات. قوله: (والدعاء المستجاب وقت الإقامة) أي الجمعة، أو الجمعة المفسرة على الصحيح بأنها من خروج الإمام إلى فراغه من الصلاة. قوله: (إذا كان يسمع) بأن كان قريباً. قوله: (إن كتابة من لا يسمع) أي البعيد. قوله: (غير ممتنعة) المعتمد المنع. قوله: (لأنه يلجئهم إلى ما نهوا عنه) وهو الكلام، وهذا إنما يظهر أن لو أطلق في الكلام أما لو قيد بالدنيوي فلا يظهر لأن هذا أخروي، وهو مما لا خلاف في إباحته كما عن العناية وغيرها وهذا البحث كثير الخلاف جداً. قوله (والمروي من سلامه) أي الإمام حين يستقر على أعلى المنبر كما فعله. قوله: (غير مقبول) لما قال البيهقي أنه ليس بقوي، وقال عبد الحق في الأحكام الكبرى هو مرسل، وهو ليس بحجة عند الشافعي رضي الله عنه، أي فكيف يستدل به عنده؟ وقوله عندنا متعلق بمقبول، أو متعلق بقوله، والمروي فإن الحدادي وجماعة من مشايخنا قالوا إنه يسلم. قوله: (وكره لمن تجب عليه الجمعة) أطلق الكراهة، فتكون تحريمية، وأخرج من لا تجب عليه فلا كراهة في خروجه. قوله: (وقيل الثاني) هذا الخلاف مبني على الخلاف في وجوب السعي بالأول أو بالثاني. قوله: (ما لم يصل الجمعة) على الصحيح كما في شرح المنية، والمسافر إذا دخل مصر أو لم ينو إقامة نصف شهر لا جمعة عليه، وإن عزم على أن يمكث فيه يومها بخلاف القروي العازم، فإنه يلحق بأهل المصر وإن نوى الخروج من يومه ولو بعد الزوال لا تلزمه الجمعة هكذا قال الفقيه وقيل: إن دخل الوقت قبل خروجه من المصر لزمته الجمعة مطلقاً كذا في الخلاصة. قال البرهان الحلبي، ولم يذكر قاضيخان إلا عدم لزومها إذا نوى الخروج من يومه قبل الوقت أو بعده، كما اختار الفقيه أبو الليث فعلم أنه المختار عنده لأنه إذا نوى إقامة ذلك اليوم في المصر.
Book of Prayer. The Friday sermon: he says the takbir, and they differed regarding his sitting when he is silent. According to Abu Yusuf it is permissible, and according to Muhammad it is not permissible, because the prohibition is due to violating the obligation of listening, and there is no listening here, and there is an absolute command. And when the khatib commands sending blessings upon the Prophet silently, to obtain both virtues. And he praises silently if he sneezes, according to the correct view. In al-Yanabi', it is disliked to say tasbih, recite Quran, or send blessings upon the Prophet if he hears. It is narrated from Nasir ibn Yahya that if one is far from the imam, he may recite Quran. It is narrated that he used to move his lips and recite Quran. Whoever does likewise and does not disturb others with the sound of his recitation, there is no harm, like looking in a book or writing. There is a difference of opinion, and it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that there is no harm. Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad said: No one more knowledgeable than al-Hakam ibn Zuhayr entered Iraq. Al-Hakam used to sit with Abu Yusuf on Friday, look in his book and correct with a pen during the sermon. And he does not return greeting nor respond to a sneezer, because he is occupied with obligatory listening. In al-Hujja, Abu Hanifa disliked responding to a sneezer. Himself: Quran, prayer, tasbih, writing and the like. Rather, he must listen and be silent. In Sharh al-Zahidi, what is disliked in prayer is disliked for the listener of the sermon: eating, drinking, playing, turning away, and the like. In al-Khulasa, everything that is prohibited in the sacred precinct is prohibited during the sermon, even commanding good. In al-Sayyid, listening to the sermon from beginning to end is obligatory, even if it mentions the rulers, and this is the most correct. Likewise, listening to other sermons like the marriage sermon and the concluding sermon. They differed regarding being close to the imam; the correct answer is that it is better. Many scholars said being distant is better so as not to hear praise of the oppressors and prayer for them. And he sits in the first row next to the imam without causing harm. His saying: (Because the prohibition) is the reason for the basic difference, and for the statement of Abu Yusuf permitting it in sitting as well. His saying: (he prays secretly) such that he hears, as al-Qahistani stated. In the commentary from al-Husami, he prays in himself. In al-Fath, from Abu Yusuf: he should do it in himself because it does not distract him from hearing the sermon, thus obtaining both virtues, and that is correct. His saying: (and he praises in himself). When the sermon ends, he praises with his tongue, just as if he hears wind, he answers in his heart, and when it ends he answers with his tongue, as in al-Muhit. His saying: (and there is a difference of opinion) and the relied upon is prohibition. In al-Walwalijiyya, al-Nasa'i: if the khatib responds, not the sermon, he should not recite Quran but listen and be silent. That is the chosen. His saying: (and al-Hasan etc.) the relied upon of the school is prohibition. He said in al-Kanz: rather he should listen and be silent, and the forgetful is like the near. His saying: (and al-Hakam) with kasrah of 'in'. His saying: (and he does not return greeting) absolutely, not with his tongue nor his heart, neither before finishing nor after, because this greeting is not permitted legally; rather, by his greeting he commits sin because it disturbs the listener's mind from the obligatory. His saying: (and he does not respond to a sneezer etc.) Should he praise if he sneezes? The correct is yes, in himself. And if he did not speak with his tongue but gestured with his head, hand, or eye to remove an evil or answer a questioner, it is not disliked, according to the correct view as in al-Mudmarat and al-Fath. His saying: (because he hears) and returning greeting when the imam comes out until he finishes his prayer, as we presented earlier. This does not include warning and calling out due to fear for a blind person falling into a well or fear of a snake or scorpion, because the right of a human takes precedence over silence for the right of Allah. The responsive supplication during the iqamah is done with the heart, not the tongue. And it is disliked for the attendee of the sermon to eat and drink. Al-Kamal said it is prohibited, even if it is commanding good, tasbih, eating, drinking, and writing. End quote: meaning if, as we presented, writing by one who does not hear the sermon is not prevented. And it is disliked to play and turn away. So he avoids what he avoids in prayer. And the khatib should not greet the people when he ascends the pulpit because it forces them to what they are forbidden. The narrated greeting from him is not accepted among us. And it is disliked for the one upon whom Friday is obligatory to leave the city on Friday after the call (i.e., the first adhan, and it is said the second) as long as he has not prayed the Friday prayer, because the command to hasten applies before it is actualized by walking. If he leaves before zawal, there is no harm according to our scholars. Similarly after finishing it even if he did not catch it. And those upon whom Friday is not obligatory, like the sick, traveler, slave, woman, blind, and disabled, if they perform it, it suffices for the obligatory prayer. His saying: (we presented from his saying 'when the imam comes out etc.'). His saying: (and it is not from it) i.e., from the disliked speech. His saying: (the right of Allah) is a substitute for silence. His saying: (and the responsive supplication during the iqamah) i.e., Friday, or interpreted correctly as from the imam's coming out until the end of the prayer. His saying: (if he hears) meaning if he is near. His saying: (that writing by one who does not hear) i.e., the distant. His saying: (is not prevented) the relied upon is prohibition. His saying: (because it forces them to what they are forbidden) i.e., speech. This is apparent only if speech is unrestricted. But if it is restricted to worldly matters, it is not apparent because this is otherworldly, and there is no difference of opinion regarding its permissibility, as in al-Inaya and others. This research is very disputed. His saying (and the narrated greeting from him) i.e., the imam when he settles at the top of the minbar as he did. His saying: (is not accepted) because al-Bayhaqi said it is not strong, and Abd al-Haqq in al-Ahkam al-Kubra said it is mursal, and it is not a proof according to al-Shafi'i, so how can one use it as proof? And his saying 'among us' is connected to 'accepted' or to 'narrated'. Al-Haddadi and a group of our scholars said that he does greet. His saying: (and it is disliked for the one upon whom Friday is obligatory) he used the absolute dislike, so it is prohibitive. And he excluded those upon whom it is not obligatory, so there is no dislike in their leaving. His saying: (and it is said the second) this difference is based on the difference regarding the obligation of hastening with the first or second adhan. His saying: (as long as he has not prayed Friday) is the correct view as in Sharh al-Munyah. The traveler who enters a city but does not intend to stay half a month has no Friday obligation. If he intends to stay on that day, he is like the villager who intends to leave on that day; if he intends to leave on the same day even after zawal, Friday is not obligatory upon him. Thus said al-Faqih. It was said: if the time enters before he leaves the city, Friday becomes obligatory upon him absolutely, as in al-Khulasa. Al-Burhan al-Halabi said: Qadikhan only mentioned it not being obligatory if he intends to leave on that day before or after the time, as al-Faqih Abu al-Layth chose, so it is known that it is the chosen view because if he intends to stay that day in the city.
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiyah) on the rulings of the Friday khutbah, addressing several intra-Hanafi disputes and clarifying the relied-upon positions. The commentary opens by noting a disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the imam’s sitting during the silent pause in the khutbah. Abu Yusuf permits it, while Muhammad prohibits it because the prohibition is tied to violating the obligation of listening; during that pause there is no listening, so the prohibition lifts. The author then discusses the permissibility of reciting Quran, tasbih, and blessings upon the Prophet during the sermon, with conditions based on distance from the khatib. The relied-upon view is that one should not engage in any such acts if he can hear the sermon; if he is far, it is permitted. The text also covers the prohibition of returning greetings and responding to sneezes, with the correct view being that one should only praise Allah silently in the heart. The prohibition applies absolutely, though gesturing to remove a harm is allowed. Further, eating, drinking, and writing during the sermon are disliked (or prohibited according to some). The khatib should not greet the congregation when ascending the pulpit to avoid causing them to speak. The passage concludes with the ruling on leaving the city on Friday after the adhan: it is prohibitively disliked for those obliged to attend Friday prayer, unless they have already prayed. Those not obliged (sick, traveler, etc.) may leave without dislike. The discussion includes the difference between the first and second adhan and the conditions for travelers. The overall tenor is that the khutbah demands full attention, and actions that distract are either prohibited or disliked.

Key Terms
الخطبة (al-khutbah): The Friday sermon delivered by the imam before the Friday prayer.
تشميت العاطس (tashmit al-ʻāṭis): Responding to a sneezer by saying 'yarhamukallah'.
النداء (al-nidāʼ): The adhan (call to prayer) indicating the time for Friday prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on eating and drinking during the Friday khutbah according to the relied-upon Hanafi position?
A) Permissible (mubah)
B) Recommended (sunnah)
C) Disliked (makruh tahrimi)
D) Obligatory (wajib)

Key Takeaway
During the Friday khutbah, silence and attentiveness are wajib; any verbal or physical distraction is prohibited unless it involves saving a life or similar urgent need.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate

Invalidation of Zuhr Prayed Before Jumu'ah Upon Striving to Attend
Commentary on multiple Jumu'ah rulings including salams, leaving city, and prior zuhr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of zuhr prayed before Jumu'ah and apply the condition under which it becomes invalid (batal) upon striving (saʿy) to attend Jumu'ah, including for the excused person (maʿdhūr).

Core Idea
Praying zuhr before Jumu'ah is conditionally valid; leaving your house to attend Jumu'ah invalidates it, even if you do not catch the prayer.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi refines a critical Jumu'ah ruling: what happens when someone prays zuhr early on Friday and then decides to go to Jumu'ah? The Hanafi school holds that the zuhr becomes nafl (invalid as obligation) the moment he leaves his house with the intention to join Jumu'ah, whether he catches it or not. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the intra-madhhab and inter-madhhab differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial contract (inʿiqād) of zuhr
Understand that zuhr is the original obligation (al-aṣl). Its time begins at zawal. If a person prays zuhr before Jumu'ah, the prayer is contracted (inʿaqada) but its final validity is suspended because the duty to attend Jumu'ah overrides.
This is a conditional validity — it remains valid only if he does not later strive to Jumu'ah.
2
Define the trigger (sabab) of invalidation
The act of striving (saʿy) means leaving the house with the intention to attend Jumu'ah. According to the relied-upon (muftā bihi) opinion, invalidation occurs upon leaving the house, even if the imam is not yet present.
Some scholars say after two steps, but the 'most correct' (al-aṣaḥḥ) view is upon departure.
3
Apply to the excused person (maʿdhūr)
Those excused from Jumu'ah (e.g., sick, traveler) may pray zuhr at home. If they later decide to attend Jumu'ah and strive, their zuhr also becomes nafl. This is the Hanafi position.
The excused person's Jumu'ah counts as his obligatory prayer, invalidating his earlier zuhr.
4
Compare the opposing view
Imam Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that the excused person's zuhr remains valid and Jumu'ah becomes supererogatory for him. The dispute hinges on whether the obligation of Jumu'ah can retroactively affect an earlier valid act.
The practical consequence (thamarah) is that according to Hanafis, the excused person must repeat zuhr if he catches Jumu'ah; according to the others, he does not.

Source Text
